Until now, **[despite increasing attention to
relationality]{.underline}** in the social sciences
**[and]{.underline}** emerging interest in **[quantum theory in
IR]{.underline}** (Der Derian, 2013; Wendt, 2015), **[[holographic
relationality remains understudied]{.underline}]{.mark}** (Milovanovic,
2013, 1). Wendt's book (2015) briefly examines the state as a
holographic system. But in his own words, that book 'is all philosophy',
rather than IR (Wendt, 2015, 2). Yet, **[[holographic ontology provides
a new and exciting way of theorizing IR and doing IR
research.]{.mark}]{.underline}** As Wendt and Duvall (1989, 55) note,
**[social ontologies 'have conceptual and methodological consequences
for how theorists approach those phenomena they seek to
explain']{.underline}**. **[[From]{.mark} the [holograph]{.mark}ic
relational ontolog[y]{.mark}, [I draw]{.mark} the following [conceptual
and methodological implications for IR]{.mark} in general and
[for]{.mark} understanding [China]{.mark}'s rise [in
particular]{.mark}.]{.underline}**4 These implications do not amount to
a full-fledged theory on China or IR; rather, they intend to exemplify
some broad promises of 'quantum theory as an interpretive tool for the
social sciences' (Der Derian, 2013, 583n7). In this spirit, I hope
**[[this can help generate further research in this still largely
uncharted terrain.]{.mark}]{.underline}** First of all, **[[to the
extent that i]{.mark}nternational [r]{.mark}elations [contain
parts]{.mark}]{.underline}** (such as states) **[[and
wholes]{.underline}]{.mark}** (such as international society and the
global economy), **[[their relations are
holographic]{.underline}]{.mark}** relations. **[[As such,
states]{.underline}]{.mark}** (or non-state actors for that matter)
**[[are not fixed]{.mark} or essentialist things with their own
intrinsic properties.]{.underline}** **[[We can still talk about states
and their characteristics,]{.mark} [but]{.mark} to make good sense of
them, [we need to look at their holographic roots]{.mark} in their wider
worlds [and understand how such entanglements enable a state to become
'the way it is', not as an essential being, but always as holographic
becoming]{.mark}]{.underline}**. All states necessarily emerge from, and
contain information of, multiple worlds, and in this sense, their
relations are not merely inter-national relations or foreign relations.
With world politics grounding in a holographic ontology, **[[it is
possible and indeed imperative to perceive and practice IR in a more
dynamic, holistic, and cooperative way]{.underline}]{.mark}**. 'Foreign
policy' as a concept, no doubt befitting a Cartesian/Newtonian ontology
of self and Other, may give way to global public policy of empathy and
shared responsibility in dealing with challenges arising from such
holographic relations. **[[Poststructuralist, postcolonial, and feminist
IR literature has usefully deconstructed the essentialist, binary
oppositions]{.underline}]{.mark}** of identity/difference, self/Other,
inside/outside, and West/Rest. **[[But]{.mark} [the holographic ontology
provides a new perspective on why such dichotomies are fundamentally
untenable given their inevitably common holographic
entanglements]{.mark}.]{.underline}** This does not mean that all states
are like-units (Waltz, 1979). The world is not an ideal-type holographic
system within which each part equally shares the same properties of
their holographic whole. Second, insofar as holographic relations in
global politics are constituted largely through informational and
discursive connections, **[[discourse analysis can help trace the
lineage and genealogy of norms and ideas which make global society hang
together]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Thus, in a methodological sense, the
holographic relational ontology has much in common with the
poststructuralist discursive ontology. Yet, while the latter sees things
as constructed by discourse, it says little on the being of discourse
itself. **[The [holograph]{.mark}ic relational
ontolog[y]{.mark}]{.underline}**, on the other hand, **[[treats
discourse as existing in as well as constitutive of holographic
relations]{.mark}.]{.underline}** The holographic relational existence
of discourse, implied in notions such as discursive formation and
intertextuality (Merrell, 1991, 193; Pitts, 1995, 302--303), can explain
why discourse cannot be attributed to a single author and why it (and
its associated power effect) appears at once to be everywhere and
nowhere. Thanks to their holographic ontology, **[discourse and power go
capillary]{.underline}** (Foucault, 1980, 142). Third, given the
existence of holographic relations in both spatial and temporal terms,
**[[understanding a part requires paying attention to both its
holographic relationality in/with the past as well as in the present, so
as to avoid both ahistorical analysis and historicist
determinism]{.underline}]{.mark}**. History is not destiny in that we
are not bound by ancient hatred or have to mechanically repeat
time-honored great power tragedy, but nor is it something which we can
completely transcend (aka the 'End of History'). We are a holographic
part of space and time, our ontological wholes. Jawaharlal Nehru, for
example, knew too well his country's historically situated holographic
'identity':

> **"** \[T\]he history of India is a long history of her relations with
> the other countries of Asia. Streams of culture have come to India
> from the West and the East and been absorbed in India, producing the
> rich and variegated culture which is India today. At the same time,
> streams of culture have flowed from India to distant parts of Asia. If
> you would know India, you have to go to Afghanistan and West Asia, to
> Central Asia, to China and Japan and to the countries of South-East
> Asia (Nehru, 2011, 315). **"**

In short, **[while Cartesian/Newtonian-inspired theorizing favors
'simplifying']{.underline}** (Waltz, 1979, 6), **[holographic ontology
highlights a strong quantum characteristic of complexity, uncertainty,
and indeterminacy about global life]{.underline}**. Instead of a
teleological process of homogenization, holographic transition is more
multifaceted and dynamic. Moreover, **[[scholarly discourses are part
and parcel of holographic relations]{.mark} in IR, and [rather than
merely a neutral analytic tool]{.mark} independent of the world they
describe, they are an inherently participatory factor in how holographic
relations take shape and evolve]{.underline}**. Consequently,
holographic reality is necessarily protean and complex, and our
theorizing effort ought to reflect it accordingly.

## 2ac -- K -- Generic

### 2ac -- L/T -- Flow Good

#### In the face of a binaristic world structured by international relations the aff's imagination becomes an organizing point for discourse that escalates to a dissolution of Westphalia. We theorize IR as a pool -- fluid -- each world not greater than the other but a web of epistemes in which a Chinese yin and western yang are not oppositional but flow naturally and organically -- stabilizing and strengthening the whole.

L. H. M. **Ling 18**, \[L. H. M. Ling (PhD, MIT) is Associate Dean of
Faculty Affairs, The New School for Public Engagement (NSPE) and
Associate Professor in the Graduate Programme in International Affairs
(GPIA), Milano School of International Affairs, Management, and Urban
Policy, at The New School in New York City, 4-1-2018, Heart and Soul for
World Politics: Advaita Monism and Daoist Trialectics in IR, Oxford
Academic, International Relations of the Asia-Pacific, Volume 18, Issue
3, September 2018,
https://academic.oup.com/irap/article-abstract/18/3/313/4959894?redirectedFrom=fulltext\]//Lex
RA Pgs. 15 - 19

4 **[Advaitic-daoist IR: a pool of multiple worlds]{.underline}** With
water as metaphor, **[[world politics becomes a pool of fluid, multiple
worlds]{.underline}]{.mark}**.23 **[[Each world represents a node of
epistemes that ripples outward, melting at the edges with other
concentric spills of epistemes.]{.mark}]{.underline}** These circulate
communal modes of thinking and doing, being and relating that have
evolved over millennia. **[Like water, multiple worlds must flow freely
to stay vibrant; [in IR terms, this means interacting with Difference
and hybridizing with Others]{.mark}]{.underline}**.24 Nonetheless,
**[the nodes run deep: that is, multiple worlds do not lose their
distinctive characteristics]{.underline}**. These reflect and sustain
legacies of history and tradition, sentiment and practice transmitted
through language, memory, storytelling, and rituals. Indeed, in every
ancient civilization, myth accounts for the origin of these nodes. Like
raindrops from Heaven, they simply descend upon the pool one day. At the
same time, these nodes do not stay the same forever. **[Past currents
fill the present as much as present circulations wash the
past]{.underline}**. Our brief excursion into darsana, dharma, and
ayurveda indicates a sense of how epistemic border-crossings effect
transformations both communally and individually. **[[In this pool of
multiple worlds, 'China' qualifies as one node among
many]{.mark}.]{.underline}** Its size and weight may affect the current
more than most but all nodes, no matter how big or small, form
concentric circles that ripple outward to merge with others, no matter
how far or near. As mentioned, the pool's water comes from and reflects
systemic dynamics beyond the pool: e.g., the sky and other environmental
elements (like climate change) that could disturb the pool's
tranquility; ripples that evaporate over time (like stories and
memories, prayers and traditions) only to return as new raindrops (like
innovations, trends, and revolutionary developments); and other,
unforeseen events (like inter-galactic contact). Sameness (globalities)
and difference (localities) thus co-exist, copenetrate, and co-produce.
**[[The very multiplicity of nodes, ripples, and circulations in the
pool of multiple worlds ensures a mutual balancing that evens the
flow]{.underline}]{.mark}** -- if left alone as wuwei advises. With
dynamic forces at play, water can stay fresh and vital; otherwise,
stagnant water like epistemic violence/epistemicide can spread
life-threatening disease (e.g., ecological disaster, economic
depression, ceaseless warfare) through mosquitoes (e.g., corrupt
bureaucracies, predatory capitalism) or some other vermin. Still, one
centripetal force applies: the heart. How else could we awaken wisdom
and enact selfless compassion? In Chinese medicine (zhongyi), the heart
organ 'rules' the body25; in ayurveda, as noted, the heart chakra alone
connects the lower three vortices of physical health with the upper
three of spiritual well-being. The entire system hinges on the heart.
The critical reader may interject: Where lies the heart or spirituality
in water? Here, I return to Laozi's respect for water. **[Daoism treats
[water]{.mark} as if it [has volition]{.mark}]{.underline}**. That is,
**[[water's transformative qualities]{.underline}]{.mark}** ('meekest in
the world'/'strongest in the world') **[[come from an inherent integrity
and agency]{.underline}]{.mark}** ('benefits everything', 'dwell in
places loathed by the crowd'). For this reason, water not only reflects
but also enables the dao. Like the human body, **[[our world-of-worlds
encases a multitude of life-forces operating in dynamic
tension]{.underline}]{.mark}**. The deepest, most concrete chakras of
safety, sexuality, and nourishment can rise to the higher, more abstract
ones of creativity, intuition, and cosmic consciousness, just as the
upper chakras can reach down into the lower ones. Indeed, if the
principle of wuwei presides, **[[each]{.underline}]{.mark}** chakra
**[[flows naturally and organically into the other, stabilizing and
strengthening the whole.]{.mark}]{.underline}** What does this mean in
terms of real-world policies and strategies? Let us consider the
implications of daoist water, ayurvedic chakras, yin/ yang trialectics,
advaita connectedness, and Buddhist duality with nonduality for
understanding 'China's rise'. 4.1 **[[China's rise
re-appraised]{.mark}]{.underline}** As a thought experiment, let us
designate 'China' as yin and the 'West' as yang. (The specific
designation doesn't matter since the process remains the same.) **[[With
daoist water as metaphor, we see that the initial dichotomy of 'China'
and the 'West' as two fixed binaries cannot hold]{.underline}]{.mark}**;
instead, each principal flows into the other in all ways and at all
levels, ranging from the 'micro' to the 'macro'. **[[A trialectical
effect takes place, highlighting a heterotopic-third domain that
connects the principals while creating a new
hybrid.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Zen Buddhism's five-rank protocol shows
how: 1. The first two ranks recognize that **[[a China-yin exists within
a West-yang]{.mark} [as much as a West-yang exists in a
China-yin.]{.mark} One prominent example comes from immigrant
communities in both places.]{.underline}** For now, let us date these to
the 19th-century: say, the Chinese diaspora in the West and Western
missionaries/merchants/expatriates in China. Each group may not only
assimilate into its new environment but both also retain their
distinctive features. **[Here, [we begin to break down the nationalistic
confines of the Westphalian state]{.mark} [to recognize the
transnational communities that make world
politics]{.mark}.]{.underline}** These include neighboring sites like
Southeast Asia that also have a longstanding Chinese diaspora and,
similarly, British colonial 'ex-pats' in South and Southeast Asia. In
this way, we unlock the Westphalian trap of prioritizing powerful 'Great
Gamers' to the exclusion and at the expense of the rest of the planet.
Breaking this colonial bondage enables us to pay attention to crucial
connections with and across the global South. 'Epistemic violence' and
'epistemicide' begin to dissipate. 2. The third rank posits that the two
principals, **[[China-yin and West-yang, can come together.
Communication becomes key]{.mark}. [Not only would the two communities
have the most at stake to engage with each other directly but also with
compassion]{.mark}.]{.underline}** Elsewhere (Ling and Pinheiro,
forthcoming), **[[we develop a model of dialogue called Creative
Listening and Speaking]{.underline}]{.mark}** (CLS). **[[It mandates
listening with the courage to speak and speaking with the humility to
listen; otherwise, neither listening nor speaking alone could dismantle
hegemony]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Again, commonalities surge forward,
transforming pre-existing conflicts and contradictions into a
trialectical heterotopia.26 3. **[The fourth rank urges action based on
this insight. [We do not need to list here specific policies and/or
strategies]{.mark}. [The two principals' internal
entwinements]{.mark}]{.underline}** (yin-within-yang, yang-within-yin)
**[[can guide action based on the imaginations and interests of the
communities themselves and in their own context rather than anything an
outsider or lofty principles could provide]{.mark}.]{.underline}** The
fourth rank underscores, also, the value and relevance of these
transnational communities for the state. They have the
**[[expertise]{.mark} in language, cultural knowledge, and assimilative
experiences [to help the state transcend hackneyed dichotomies like
'China' versus the 'West'.]{.mark}]{.underline}** 4. The fifth rank
affirms 'unity attained'. Perhaps a ceremonious event may suffice but it
performs a necessary function to declare 'there is no more duality'. The
event, moreover, will need to resonate contextually to attain its full
meaning; otherwise, community members will (rightfully) regard it as a
cynical move to mask hegemony in 'friendlier, gentler' guise.27
**[[Water as metaphor offers innovative strategies for inter-state
conflict]{.mark} as well]{.underline}**. For instance, an ayurvedic lens
on China's regional conflicts would attribute these to a fixation with
the lower chakras only, thereby forcing hostilities due to a classic
(in)security dilemma. **[[Each state seeks to protect or enhance its own
security (safety) by making hypermasculine-militaristic poses
(sexuality) that spiral everyone's sense of
insecurity]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Meanwhile, the population's ability
to feed itself (nourishment) suffers, as national security consumes more
and more resources, even as the state claims to protect all aspects of
the supposed 'heartland'. However, the upper chakras can guide the lower
ones to reach a healthier, happier, more sustainable outcome. The fifth
chakra of creativity, for example, could inspire use of the arts and
humanities to broaden, not to mention deepen, sovereign relations among
peoples in both states (Ling and Nakamura, 2017). The governments and
societies of China and Japan could re-appreciate what each has meant to
the other from ancient times to 'catch-up' modernity (Ling, 2016b). With
two millennia of history together, China and Japan cannot -- should not
-- risk everything on the past 200 years. **[[The region, furthermore,
abounds with strategies for conflict resolution that pre-date
Westphalia]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Chong, 2012). **[[Why not draw on
these precedents?]{.mark}]{.underline}** The highest chakras help these
strategies reach fruition. With intuition and cosmic consciousness, we
gain a sense of 'awakened wisdom and selfless compassion'. **[[We have a
reason, method, and ideal to dissolve Westphalia's deadly deadlock of
narrow, nationalistic interests set in immutable, binary
oppositions]{.underline}]{.mark}**. **[[From this basis, world politics
no longer divides into Self-aggrandizing, hypermasculinized 'Great
Gamers' versus invisibilized, feminized 'small fry'. From 'epistemic
violence' and/or 'epistemicide']{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[[world
politics can embark, instead, on engagement, learning, and mutuality:
that is, Interbeing.]{.underline}]{.mark}**

## 2ac -- K -- Identity

### 2ac -- General Defense against ks of staticism \[Identity\]

#### Link Turn -- the aff embraces a form of Advaita monism -- that runs counter to Hegelian and Marxist ontological assumptions -- we dissolve the ability to "know china" in the IR context.

L. H. M. **Ling 18**, \[L. H. M. Ling (PhD, MIT) is Associate Dean of
Faculty Affairs, The New School for Public Engagement (NSPE) and
Associate Professor in the Graduate Programme in International Affairs
(GPIA), Milano School of International Affairs, Management, and Urban
Policy, at The New School in New York City, 4-1-2018, Heart and Soul for
World Politics: Advaita Monism and Daoist Trialectics in IR, Oxford
Academic, International Relations of the Asia-Pacific, Volume 18, Issue
3, September 2018,
https://academic.oup.com/irap/article-abstract/18/3/313/4959894?redirectedFrom=fulltext\]//Lex
RA Pgs. 4 - 8

**[China's rise: implications for IR]{.underline}** Today's China still
disturbs. It may no longer sponsor anti-Western revolution at home or
abroad, and has transformed, instead, into a bastion of neoliberal
capitalism for almost forty years. **[But [the People's Republic still
unsettles the West]{.mark} mainly [due to its integration, or lack
thereof, into the 'international community']{.mark}]{.underline}**
(Economist, 25 January 2018). What exactly, many worry, is or will be
China's role in world politics? **[[To Liberal
Westphalians]{.underline}]{.mark}** like Barry Buzan (2010) and G. John
Ikenberry (2011), **[a [rising China could destabilize]{.mark} or
consolidate the status quo, depending on the willingness of leaders in
Beijing to cooperate with the Western-led, liberal world order -- and
how much the West can insist on or entice such
cooperation.]{.underline}** To Chinese Westphalians like Yan Xuetong
(2011), China will invariably alter the status quo regardless of
leadership inclinations. The country's demographic and territorial size,
not to mention its historical import, will make a difference. Another
source of convergence comes from Classical Westphalians and Chinese
Constructivists. The former, like Henry Kissinger (2011), propose that
China and the West should just get together, like the Pope and the Kings
of Europe did in the 16th-century. They divided the world among
themselves, then ruled accordingly. Stability and order ensued -- at
least for the principals involved. (That such comity initiated five
centuries of violence and brutality against those not privy to the
bargain seems to have escaped Classical Westphalians -- or they don't
care.) Chinese Constructivists like Qin Yaqing (2010, 2011, 2016) and
other members of the 'Chinese School of IR' (Zhang and Chang 2016) make
a slightly different argument. Chinese norms and values, they believe,
will naturally interact with the world to effect changes that remain, as
yet, unanticipated. Together with the West, Qin suggests, China will
form a new global order. 2.1 Critique **[[Neither camp questions
categories]{.underline}]{.mark}** **[[like 'China', the 'West', the
'interstate system', or even the 'Westphalian state']{.mark}. [All
presume that states will remain the central unit of IR analysis,
functioning as self-enclosed, self-interested units of
power]{.mark}.]{.underline}** Even Qin Yaqing imposes a
statist--nationalist frame onto a philosophical and normative tradition
-- yin/yang dynamics -- that had no such intention or purpose in the
first place.6 Despite the systemic dynamics that he himself has
introduced, **[[anything outside the China/West matrix remains an
afterthought or a playground for the powerful]{.underline}]{.mark}**.
Yan Xuetong puts it more directly: so far, only 'small fry' like Vietnam
and the Philippines have complained about China's moves in the South
China Sea -- meaning there is no "real" opposition from a major power
like the United States, Europe, or Russia (Huang, 2016). So China has
nothing to worry about. (He conveniently omits China's standoff with
Japan in the East China Sea.) Both camps also unite in their patriarchal
proclivities (Ling, 2016c). **[Whether it's Brotherly Love under
Confucian tianxia]{.underline}** (All-underHeaven) **[or a more perfect,
machine-like Artificial Man in Hobbes' Leviathan, the Chinese School and
their Westphalian counterparts partake in what is effectively a
gentleman's club.]{.underline}** Some, like the English school, may
appear in top hat and morning coat; others, like the Chinese school, may
dress in changpao magua. 7 But all revel in a common, hypermasculine
camaraderie: that is, they regale one another with clever chatter or
perhaps a zinger or two while being served, silently and efficiently, by
those whom they will never know or even acknowledge but whose labor and
resources make their 'club' possible in the first place. **[[Not
surprisingly, these hypermasculine theorists discount anything smacking
of the non-masculine]{.underline}]{.mark}** (e.g.,
postcolonial-feminism), **[[non-heteronormative]{.underline}]{.mark}**
(e.g., **[[queer theory]{.underline}]{.mark}**), **[[and especially
non-secular]{.mark}]{.underline}** (e.g., '**[[gods and
spirits']{.underline}]{.mark}**).8 **[[There is no room in the IR club
for such 'distractions'.]{.mark} [Herein lies the nub of Self-delusion
in Westphalian IR.]{.mark} [History has shown]{.mark}, time and again,
[that society's so-called 'servile' and 'inferior' classes can make new
worlds despite generations of racism--sexism--imperialism entrenched by
hierarchy and privilege,]{.mark} colonialism and
imperialism]{.underline}** (Kataneksza et al., 2018). As Andean activist
Humberto **[[Cholango declared to Pope Benedict XVI in a letter in
2007]{.underline}]{.mark}**, '**[[we]{.underline}]{.mark}** \[the Andean
people\] **[[are still here']{.underline}]{.mark}** (Cholango quoted in
Cadena, 2010, p. 335). **[[Despite centuries of
control]{.underline}]{.mark}**, if not genocide, the people of **[[the
Andes -- like other feminized, colonized subjects throughout the globe
-- have survived and they did so by 'learn\[ing\] how to merge our
beliefs and symbols with the ones of the invaders and
oppressors']{.underline}]{.mark}** (Cholango quoted in Cadena, 2010, p.
334). Such is epistemic compassion.9 **[I [turn]{.mark} now [to advaita
monism and daoist trialectics for how--
what--why.]{.mark}]{.underline}** 3 **[[Advaita monism and daoist
trialectics]{.underline}]{.mark}**: **[[connectedness and
compassion]{.mark}]{.underline}** 3.1 **[Advaita monism]{.underline}**
Most cite Shankara (c. 8th century BCE) for articulating advaita. It
relates to but differs from two other branches of the subcontinent's
vedas ('knowledge'): dvaita (dualism) and vishishtadvaita (qualified
monism or holism).the **[[The concept of Advaita]{.underline}]{.mark}**
(literally meaning non-dual or nonsecondness) **[[pre-supposes a monist
epistemology that]{.underline}]{.mark}**\...**[[ties
the]{.underline}]{.mark}** perceiver
(**[[subject]{.underline}]{.mark}**) **[[and the]{.underline}]{.mark}**
perceived (**[[object]{.underline}]{.mark}**) **[[together with a
globe]{.mark}]{.underline}** marked with 'single hidden connectedness'
or Brahman (Shahi and Ascione, 2015, p. 2). From this basis, Shahi and
Ascione construct **[[an advaitic-monist model]{.mark}]{.underline}** of
world politics. It **[[suggests an 'ever-transient' but 'perpetually
connected' global system]{.underline}]{.mark}** (Shahi and Ascione,
2015, p. 15). Just as 'theorist' and 'theory' fuse into an ultimate
reality encompassed by Brahma so, too, **[advaitic-monist IR would
highlight 'an unbreakable and irreversible "micro--macro linkage" or
ontological nexus between diverse individuals, nation-states and the
world.]{.underline}**..' (Shahi and Ascione, 2015, p. 15). They
conclude: The intellectual realization of 'connectedness' can make a
powerful case for reinterpreting diversities in political identities,
thereby creating new ethical space for condemning divisive domestic,
international and global politics (Shahi and Ascione, 2015, p. 15). I
applaud this articulation of an advaitic-monist IR. **[[It brings us
closer to a world politics beyond Westphalia. Dichotomies like 'China'
versus the 'West' begin to dissolve.]{.underline}]{.mark}** Advaita
monism's dynamic, perpetual connectedness alerts us to the ties that
bind even when conflicts and contradictions seem to pull us apart.
Accordingly, **[[our analysis of China and the West cannot abide by IR
conventions]{.mark} like the three 'levels of analysis']{.underline}**
(Waltz, 1954) **[or 'structure versus units']{.underline}** (Waltz,
1979). We need to consider how all the 'constituents of the globe'
(Shahi and Ascione, 2015, p. 15) interrelate and interact. Even if
national governments may quarrel, various actors
('individuals,\...classes, communities, cultures, peoples') as well as
the context in which they operate ('ecology and the world') still have a
mediating impact, 'reveal\[ing\] the hidden connectedness across
diversities' (Shahi and Ascione, 2015, p. 15). Yet, **[[this
intellectual and heuristic agenda]{.underline}]{.mark}**, as Shahi and
Ascione (2015, p. 15) underscore, **[[remains an 'unrealized
intellectual quest'. It compels further
exploration]{.underline}]{.mark}**. I could not agree more. Nonetheless,
I raise a cautionary note. We need to resist falling into the Cartesian
trap of highlighting advaita monism in contradistinction to its two
partners in philosophy: dvaita dualism and vishishtadvaita holism.
Analytically, each branch of thought could not be without the others.
Note, for example, how people live their lives, demonstrating the
fluidity between these categories fixed by Western social science. I
focus on three cases, in particular: darsana, 10 dharma, 11 and
ayurveda.12 The first two show how advaita--dvaita--vishishtadvaita
cross epistemic borders between the divine and the mundane; the last,
the body and the mind. All three link the individual with the community,
the environment with the cosmopolitical. **[These underscore humanity's
undeniable thirst for spirituality, not just ethics. [Epistemic
compassion thus takes place]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

## 2ac ---Specific Ks

### 2ac -- Anti-Blackness

#### The kritik has the wrong starting point because it focuses solely on the social relations which uphold antiblackness without interrogating what produces those relations in the first place -- antiblackness is reified by the actions of bodies who affectively invest in racial signifiers which they uphold. The impact is ontological gridlock. That limits the radical possibilities of becomings for the black identity and encases it within a structural container -- this is our definition of how violence happens along the Westphalian hierarchy. 

Michelle **Koerner '12** \[Michelle, Professor of Comparative Literature
at UC-Berkeley, "Line of Escape: Gilles Deleuze's Encounter with George
Jackson" *Genre*, Vol. 44, No. 2 Summer 2011 DOI
10.1215/00166928-1260183/\]//Lex RA

**[In "The Case of Blackness" Moten]{.underline}** (2008b: 187)
perceptively **[remarks,]{.underline}** "**[[What is inadequate to
blackness is]{.mark} already [given ontologies]{.mark}]{.underline}**."
What if we were **[[to think of blackness as]{.mark} a name for [an
ontology of becoming]{.mark}]{.underline}**? How
**[[might]{.underline}]{.mark}** such a thinking **[[transform]{.mark}
our understanding of [the relation of blackness to history and
its]{.mark} specific [capacity to "think \[its\] way out of the
exclusionary constructions" of history]{.mark}]{.underline}** and the
thinking of history (Moten 2008a: 1744)? **[[Existing ontologies]{.mark}
tend to [reduce blackness to a historical
condition]{.mark},]{.underline}** **[a "lived experience," [and]{.mark}
in doing so effectively [eradicate its unruly character]{.mark} as a
transformative force.]{.underline}** **[Deleuze and
Guattari]{.underline}**, I think, offer a compelling way to
**[think]{.underline}** of **[this unruliness]{.underline}** when they
write, "**[What [History grasps]{.mark} of the event is]{.underline}**
its effectuation in states of affairs or in **[[lived experience, but
the event in its becoming]{.mark}, in its specific
consistency]{.underline}**, in its self- positing as concept,
**[[escapes History]{.mark}"]{.underline}** (Deleuze and Guattari 1994:
110). **[[To bring this relation]{.mark} between blackness and
becoming]{.underline}** further into the open **[--- [toward]{.mark} an
[affirmation]{.mark}]{.underline}** [**[of the
unexpected]{.underline}**]{.mark} insinuation of blackness signaled by
the use of Jackson's line as an "**[event in its
becoming]{.underline}**" --- a few more words need be said about
Deleuze's method. The use of Jackson's writing is just one instance of a
procedure that we find repeated throughout Capitalism and Schizophrenia,
where **[we constantly encounter unexpected injections of]{.underline}**
quotations, names, and **[ideas]{.underline}** lifted from other texts,
**[lines that appear all of sudden as though propelled by their own
force.]{.underline}** One might say they are deployed rather than
explained or interpreted; as such, they produce textual events that
readers may choose to ignore or pick up and run with. **[Many
names]{.underline}** are proposed for this method ---
"**[schizoanalysis]{.underline}**, **[micropolitics]{.underline}**,
**[pragmatics]{.underline}**, diagrammatism,
**[rhizomatics]{.underline}**, **[cartography]{.underline}**" (Deleuze
and Parnet \[1977\] 2006: 94) --- **[but the crucial issue is to [affirm
an experimental practice that opposes itself to]{.mark} the
[interpretation]{.mark}]{.underline}** of texts, **[proposing
[instead]{.mark} that [we think of]{.mark}]{.underline}** a book as "a
little machine" and ask "**[[what it functions
with]{.underline}]{.mark}**, in connection with what other things does
it **[[or does it not transmit intensities?"]{.underline}]{.mark}**
(Deleuze and Guattari \[1980\] 1987: 4).8 Studying how Soledad Brother
functions in Deleuze's books, connecting Jackson's line to questions and
historical issues that are not always explicitly addressed in those
books, involves one in this action. And further, it opens new lines
where the intensities transmitted in Jackson's book make a claim on our
own practice. **[This method can be seen as an effort to disrupt the
hierarchical opposition between theory and practice]{.underline}** and
to challenge some of the major assumptions of Western Marxism. In an
interview with Antonio Negri in the 1990s, Deleuze (1997: 171) clarifies
that he and Guattari have "remained Marxists" in their concern **[to
analyze the ways capitalism has developed]{.underline}** but that their
political philosophy **[makes three crucial distinctions
with]{.underline}** respect to more **[traditional theoretical
approaches]{.underline}**: first, a thinking of "war machines" as
opposed to state theory; second, **[a "consideration of minorities
rather than classes"]{.underline}**; and finally, **[the study of social
"lines of flight" rather than the interpretation and critique of social
contradictions]{.underline}**. Each of these distinctions, as we will
see, resonates with Jackson's political philosophy, but as the passage
from Anti-Oedipus demonstrates, the concept of **[the "line of flight"
emerges directly in connection]{.underline}** to Deleuze and Guattari's
encounter **[with Soledad Brother]{.underline}**. **[[The concept
affirms]{.mark} those [social constructions]{.mark} that would
[neither]{.mark} be [determined by preexisting structures nor caught
in]{.mark} a [dialectical contradiction. It names a force]{.mark} that
is [radically autonomous from existing ontologies,]{.mark} structures,
and historical accounts]{.underline}**. **[It is]{.underline}** above
all **[for this reason]{.underline}** that **[D]{.underline}**eleuze
**[and G]{.underline}**uattari **[insist that [society]{.mark} be
[thought of not as]{.mark} a "[structure" but as]{.mark} a
"[machine,"]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[[because such a concept enables
the thinking of]{.mark} the [movements]{.mark}, energies, [and
intensities]{.mark}]{.underline}** (i.e., **[the lines of flight) [that
such machines transmit. The thinking]{.mark} of machines [forces
us]{.mark} not only [to consider]{.mark} the social and historical labor
involved in producing society but also the [ongoing potentials of
constructing new]{.mark} types of [assemblages]{.mark}]{.underline}**
(agencement). **[[One of the key adversaries]{.mark} of this machinic
approach [is]{.mark}]{.underline}** "interpretation" and more
specifically **[structuralist interpretations of society [in terms of
contradictions]{.mark}]{.underline}**[.]{.mark} According to Deleuze and
Guattari (\[1980\] 1987: 293), **[[structuralism]{.mark} persisted in
the "submission of the line to the point" and as a result [produced a
theory of subjectivity, and]{.mark} also an account of [language]{.mark}
and the unconscious, [that could not think in]{.mark} terms of [movement
and construction]{.mark}]{.underline}**[.]{.mark} **[[Defining lines
only in relation to finite points]{.underline}]{.mark}** (the subject,
the signifier) **[[produces a calculable grid]{.mark},]{.underline}** a
structure that then appears as the hidden intelligibility of the system
and of society generally. Louis Althusser's account of the "ideological
State apparatus" as the **[[determining structure]{.mark} of
subjectivity [is]{.mark}]{.underline}** perhaps **[[the
extreme]{.underline}]{.mark}** expression of this **[[gridlocked
position]{.underline}]{.mark}** (an example we will come back to in a
later section). **[Opposed to this theoretical approach,
[diagrammatism]{.mark}]{.underline}** (to invoke one of the terms given
for this method) **[maps vectors that generate an open space and the
potentials]{.underline}** for giving consistency to the latter.9 In
other words, **[rather than tracing the hidden structures of an
intolerable system, D]{.underline}**eleuze **[and
G]{.underline}**uattari**['s method aims to map the ways out of
it.]{.underline}**

#### Specifically, the perm is black becoming. The cultural production of slavery is creolized, already rhizomatic. They uphold the West which bases itself upon an ontology of sameness, making racialized violence inevitable. Nomadic subjectivity shatters that dynamic. Further it's a modality through which non-black individuals can become not anti-black. 

Rossi **Braidotti 6** \[Professor and Director of the Centre for
Humanities at Utrecht University, "Transpositions: On Nomadic Ethics",
Polity\]//Lex RA

Rhizomic politics A multiply located, non-unitary subject position
**[and a rhizomic politics of relations is]{.underline}** also
**[recommended by]{.underline}** the Deleuzian philosopher **[Edouard
Glissant. He develops an effective rhizomatic poetics and
politics]{.underline}**, **[taking as his point of reference the
historical experience]{.underline}** **[and]{.underline}** the
**[specific location of Africans and West Indians caught in the
transatlantic slave trade]{.underline}**. **[Glissant foregrounds the
importance of memory and the productivity of mixity as the centrepieces
of his theory of Relation]{.underline}**. **[He argues that [even an
experience as devastating as slavery produces specific forms of
knowledge and subjectivization that transcend the burden of the
negative.]{.mark} The]{.underline}**re are several **[important
feature]{.underline}**s at stake in Glissant\'s remarkable position; the
first **[is the]{.underline}** **[primacy of the Relation]{.underline}**
over any of its terms, **[including the negative ones]{.underline}**.
**[A relation functions through the middle, the
\'milieu\']{.underline}** (more on this in the next chapter). **[People
who are]{.underline}** culturally and ethnically **[positioned in the
middle - like the Caribbeans or West Indians]{.underline}** - **[have a
head start in understanding the crucial importance of the
relation]{.underline}**. **[[They also have]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
however, **[[a historical legacy of destruction and violence]{.mark},
[which is hard to transcend]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[[in that it
includes]{.mark} both [the erasure of]{.mark} the [original culture
and]{.mark} the [forceful absorption of the colonizing
culture.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[[In response to
this]{.mark}]{.underline}** ethical and political **[challenge,
[Glissant actively theorizes]{.mark} the [becoming-minoritarian or
becoming-rhizomatic of blacks]{.mark}, Creoles, descendants of slaves
and colonized peoples]{.underline}**. **[This is described as [a
spiritual]{.mark} but also logistical [shift in the structure of the
subject in the direction of openness towards both self and
other.]{.mark} [Glissant\'s position includes a]{.mark} sharp [critique
of the West]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[[which is based on the ontology
of Sameness]{.underline}]{.mark}** or **[the rule of One.]{.underline}**
This includes a dualistic relationship to the rest of the human race.
There exists a dominant mode of nomadism in Western culture - in the
form of epic journeys of discovery, which find their historical apogee
in colonialism. **[The power of Sameness]{.underline}** **[in the west
is best described in terms of monolinguism, or [the illusion of a single
cultural and linguistic root]{.mark}]{.underline}**[.
**[Glissant]{.underline}**]{.mark}**[,]{.underline}** in a very
Deleuzian mode, **[[plays the rhizome]{.mark} [against the root]{.mark}
and advocates global polylinguism]{.underline}**. This includes **[the
deconstruction of the hubris of European master cultures and the
arrogance with which they consider their languages as the voice of
humanity]{.underline}**. **[[This universalistic pretence is]{.mark}
[one of the mechanisms supporting colonialism]{.mark}.]{.underline}**
**[It also entails the reappraisal of minor languages,]{.underline}**
dialects and hybrids, in a phenomenon that Glissant describes as
**[creolization: [\'Poets from the Caribbean, the Maghreb and]{.mark}
[other parts of Africa are not moving toward that elsewhere]{.mark} that
is the aim of projectile movement, nor are they returning toward a
Centre. [They create]{.mark} [their works in metropolitan
regions,]{.mark} [where their peoples have made a sudden
appearance\']{.mark}]{.underline}** (Glissant 1997: 31). **[Glissant
offers a striking example of the poetics of relation in his analysis
of]{.underline}** **[how, [in]{.mark}]{.underline}** [**[the Caribbean
colonized territories]{.underline}**]{.mark}, **[[the French
colons]{.underline}]{.mark}** **[[spoke their own homegrown
dialects]{.underline}]{.mark}** - Norman or Breton - **[[rather
than]{.mark} the high and noble language [of the]{.mark} [French
nation]{.mark}]{.underline}**[.]{.mark} **[It]{.underline}** **[is [this
bastardized language that mingles with that of the local
population]{.mark}, [creating a crossover]{.mark} between two distinct
but analogous forms of linguistic non-purity]{.underline}**.
Creolization, therefore, cuts both ways and it differs from the master
langue in its very structures. The thought of relation as a form of
philosophical nomadism stresses the importance of the middle, in this
mode of non-origin, non-purity and not-Oneness. Glissant defines this
productive multiplicity as \'echoes of the world\' - modes of resonance
of the great vitality of human biodiversity at both the biological and
the cultural level. **[They reconnect us to [the living chaos of the
world]{.mark} as living matter [in
transformation]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[a hybrid, dynamic resilient
bios/zoe force of [global creolization]{.mark}]{.underline}**[.]{.mark}
**[[Glissant captures this]{.mark} vitality and [honours it as a
poetics]{.mark} or [an ethics of rhizomatic
interconnections.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Contrary to what some
ungenerous critics suggested (Gedalof 1996, 2000; Boer 1996; Felski
1997; Pels 1999), **[[my nomadic subject pursues the same]{.mark}
[critique of power]{.mark} [as black and post-colonial
theories]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[[not in spite but because of the
fact that it is located somewhere else.]{.mark} Philosophical nomadism
addresses]{.underline}** in both a critical and creative manner t**[he
role of the former \'centre\' in redefining power relations. [Margins
and centre shift and destabilize each other]{.mark} in parallel, albeit
dissymmetrical, movements.]{.underline}** **[My position
is]{.underline}** equally **[[resistant to]{.mark} the [identification
of the centre as]{.mark} [inertia and]{.mark} self-perpetuation and to
the aporetic [repetition of Sameness]{.mark}]{.underline}**. **[[The
challenge is to destabilize]{.mark} dogmatic, [hegemonic,]{.mark}
exclusionary [power structures at the very heart of the identity
structures of the dominant subject through rhizomic
interventions]{.mark}.]{.underline}** If we are to move beyond the
sociology of travel and the breast-beating of critical thinkers crushed
by white guilt, **[[we need]{.mark} to enact a vision of the subject
that encompasses [changes in the deep structures]{.mark}]{.underline}**.
The point is not just mere deconstruction, but the relocation of
identities on new grounds that account for multiple belongings, i.e. a
non-unitary vision of a subject. This subject actively yearns for and
constructs itself in complex and internally contradictory social
relations. **[To account for these [we need to look at the internal
forms of movement that privilege processes rather
than]{.mark}]{.underline}** essences and transformations, rather than
**[[counter-claims to identity]{.underline}]{.mark}**. The
**[sociological variables]{.underline}** (**[gender, class,
race]{.underline}** and ethnicity, age, health) **[need to be
supplemented by a theory of the subject that calls into question the
inner fibres of the self.]{.underline}** **[These include the desire,
the ability and the courage to sustain multiple belongings in a context
which celebrates and rewards Sameness and one-way
thinking]{.underline}**. In chapters 4 and 51 will address this
challenge further, which is an ethical as well as a political
enterprise. It is my contribution, as a European nomadic subject moving
across the variegated landscape of whiteness, to a debate which black,
anti-racist, post-colonial and other critical thinkers have put on the
map.

#### Here's a psychoanalytic justification for our theory of race as a product of affective investment in certain signifiers. 

**Seshadri-Crooks 2K** (Kalpana, Professor of English at Boston College,
"Desiring Whiteness: A Lacanian Analysis of Race", Routledge Press 2000)

-if race only pertained to the imaginary, then it would have nothing to
do with the symbolic.

-anti-blackness doesn't structure the symbolic; the symbolic structures
anti-blackness.

Racial identification and imaginary ideology

I emphasise the register of **[the symbolic order]{.underline}** because
**[we must never lose sight of it,]{.underline}** although it is most
frequently forgotten, **[although we turn away from it in
analysis]{.underline}**. Because, in the end, what do we usually talk
about? What we go on and on about, often in a confused, scarcely
articulated fashion, are the subject's imaginary relations to the
construction of his ego. We talk all the time about the dangers, the
commotions, the crises that the subject undergoes at the level of his
ego's construction. That is why I started by explaining the relation
O-, the imaginary relation to the other. (I: 179) It is commonplace to
utilize Lacan's early essay on the mirror stage to analyze
identification as a function of ideology. **[Althusser]{.underline}**'s
much cited essay "Ideology and ideological state apparatuses" (1972),
through its allusions to Lacan, **[construes ideology as a form of
specularity]{.underline}** **[whereby the subject]{.underline}** (with a
small **[s) is subjected to the capitalized Subject in a relation of
mutual recognition]{.underline}**. **[Althusser maps this
"mirror-structure of ideology"]{.underline}** (1972:180) on a dual
plane, **[and suggests that such "duplication" constitutes the
mconnaissance that guarantees the hegemony of the ruling
classes.]{.underline}** Such an appropriation of Lacan's mirror stage
could potentially be extended to resolve the conundrum of race as a
(scientifically groundless) fraught looking. Insofar as racial
differences are understood as being inscribed on the body as skin color,
hair texture and bone structure, it seems inescapable that we should
analyze race within the paradigm of identification. In fact, Lacan's
emphasis on misrecognition as constitutive of the child's nascent ego as
a "little other" seems to lend itself to the constructionist view of
race as a "mistake" of or in the looking glass.11 **[This is a powerful
method of reading, and [Fanon is]{.mark}]{.underline}** perhaps **[the
[exemplar of such a deployment of Lacan for
race]{.mark}]{.underline}**.12 **[[In]{.underline}]{.mark}** a long
footnote in **[[Black Skin, White Masks, he speaks of racial
identification]{.mark} as it occurs in white and black children: It
would]{.underline}** indeed **[be interesting, [on the basis of
Lacan's]{.mark} theory of the [mirror period,]{.mark} to investigate the
extent to which the imago of his]{.underline}** **[fellow built up in
the young white]{.underline}** at the usual age **[would undergo an
imaginary aggression with the appearance of the Negro]{.underline}**.
**[When one has grasped the mechanism described by Lacan]{.underline}**,
**[one can have no further doubt that the real Other]{.underline}**
**[for the white man is]{.underline}** and will continue to be **[the
black man]{.underline}**. **[The Other is perceived on the level of the
body image]{.underline}**, absolutely as the not-self---that is, **[the
unidentifiable, the unassimilable]{.underline}**.
(**[Fanon]{.underline}** 1967:161) **[Just as the white child constructs
its ego]{.underline}** ideal **[on the basis of an
exclusion]{.underline}**, the child in the Antilles, who looks in the
mirror, also excludes its own color. **[[Fanon
contends]{.mark}]{.underline}** that **[when Antilleans are asked to
recall their mirror image, "invariably they reply: 'I had no
color'"]{.underline}** (1967:162). **["In the Antilles perception always
occurs on the level of the imaginary. [It is in white terms that one
perceives one's fellows]{.mark}"]{.underline}** (163). Further, "the
Antillean does not think of himself as a black man; he thinks of himself
as an Antillean" (148). **[[For Fanon,]{.mark} the [blackness]{.mark} of
the Negro's body [is a brute fact]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[[and must
found]{.mark} the subject's [identification]{.mark}]{.underline}** and
not be displaced by identification with nation or ethnicity. **[Such
misplaced identification for Fanon]{.underline}** (he terms it **[the
"mirror hallucination]{.underline}**") **[is caused by the ideological
power of]{.underline}** French cultural **[imperialism]{.underline}**.
**[Ideology then pertains to the positioning of
blackness:]{.underline}** **[for the black child, disavowal---the
inability to accept its own blackness]{.underline}**, **[and for the
white child]{.underline}**, **[phobia]{.underline}**---**[the inability
to introject the other's image.]{.underline}** **[[The above analysis of
race]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[based]{.underline}** as it is **[on a
simple reading of the mirror stage, [discloses several fundamental
problems]{.mark}]{.underline}** in the common-sense attitude towards
racial practice. **[[1 If we reduce racial practice to
racism]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[defined as power's agency to
hierarchize and discriminate]{.underline}**, **[[we must accept race as
an a priori fact]{.mark} of human difference]{.underline}**. The concept
of **[race as a system that fixates on arbitrary marks on the body
becomes neutralized,]{.underline}** **[and racism becomes the
enemy]{.underline}**. In other words, **[there is no possibility of
interrogating the structure and constitution of the subject of
race.]{.underline}** **[The question ["How]{.mark} do [we become white,
black]{.mark}, brown, or [yellow]{.mark}?"]{.underline}** **[[will be
foreclosed]{.mark}.]{.underline}** **[[We]{.underline}]{.mark}** will
**[[fail to discern racial practice]{.mark} [as stemming]{.mark} [from
race]{.mark} rather than from racism]{.underline}**.
**[[2]{.underline}]{.mark}** **[By [locating]{.mark}]{.underline}** our
reading of **[[race on]{.underline}]{.mark}** the ostensibly dual plane
**[of [the mirror relation alone]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[which leads
to the simpler opposition]{.underline}** now entrenched in cultural
studies **[between the "self" and the "other,"]{.underline}**
**[[we]{.mark} risk [confin]{.mark}ing [race to a notion of the ego as
false consciousness]{.mark}]{.underline}**. **[Race, we will then be led
to assert, is an illusory, narcissistic construct, and racism is an ego
defense.]{.underline}** [**[3]{.underline}** **[If the order of
race]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[or Whiteness [pertained only to the
subject's]{.mark} assimilation of his/her [ego
ideal]{.mark}]{.underline}**[, **[then race]{.underline}**]{.mark} as
such **[[would]{.mark} seem to [have nothing to do with the
symbolic]{.mark}]{.underline}** or the real of the unconscious, **[that
is, with the psychical structure of the subject.]{.underline}** **[It
would seem to be free of the effects of the signifier, thereby rendering
language "neutral" and free of "race."]{.underline}** As **[Fanon
implies]{.underline}**, **[racial visibility must be distinguished from
the moment]{.underline}** when **[the subject introjects an ego ideal as
a coherent body image]{.underline}**. **[But by marking the temporal
difference in the constitution of the bodily ego and the raced
body]{.underline}**, **[we will see that]{.underline}** **[the
anxiety]{.underline}** that **[Fanon refers to is not caused by the
ideology of blackness, but by the structure of Whiteness.]{.underline}**
Less cryptically: we will see that **[racial anxiety,]{.underline}**
**[the unconscious anxiety that is entailed by the sight of racial
difference]{.underline}**, **[has its cause not in
ideology]{.underline}**, **[but in the structure of race
itself,]{.underline}** **[and in the functioning of its master
signifier, "Whiteness."]{.underline}** In the following, I return to the
theory of the mirror stage, and examine the process of the integration
of the bodily image to magnify the role of the symbolic in subtending
the body image. I undertake this brief elaboration of Lacan's notion of
the imaginary, which will be familiar to many readers, to clarify my
claim that **[race cannot be mapped onto the simpler theory of
misrecognition and ego identification]{.underline}**, and that one can
do so only through an inadequate understanding of the imaginary, and of
the raced subject. The most extensive discussion of the mirror stage can
be found in Lacan's Seminar I, where he proposes, significantly, a
"substitute" for the mirror stage in an optical experiment (I: 74). In
between a concave mirror and a plane mirror, a vase out of the line of
vision is inverted below a box, with a bouquet of flowers placed upright
above it. The concave mirror reflects a "real" image which projects the
vase upright with the flowers in the vase, with the image itself seeming
to appear behind the mirror as with plane mirror images. Lacan utilizes
this fairly commonplace optical experiment, in several variations, to
characterize the mirror stage as both a "moment in development" and an
"exemplary function," that reveals "the subject's relations to his
image, insofar as it is the Urbild \[prototype\] of the ego" (I: 74,
emphasis added). In other words[, **[the mirror stage
is]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[not yet,]{.underline}** properly speaking,
**[the self-present moment of the total integration of the bodily
ego,]{.underline}** or what Lacan will term **[the ego
ideal]{.underline}**. **[Rather, it is [a]{.mark} [contingent
moment]{.mark} when the primitive "ideal ego" is projected outside as
the outline of a form that promises unity]{.underline}**. **[It is a
projection of a real image but not as yet a reintrojection of the image,
which will]{.underline}** **[necessarily have to be "libidinalized and
narcissised"]{.underline}** (I: 153) in an exchange with the image of
the other, as the bodily ego. **[[The ego ideal]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
as **[distinguished from the ideal ego]{.underline}**, **[is the site of
secondary identifications]{.underline}**, **[and is a]{.underline}**
characteristic **[function of imaginary relations]{.underline}** **[that
literally [enables the subject to see his "libidinal being"]{.mark} [in
relation to the]{.mark} [other]{.mark}]{.underline}** (I: 125--6). This
is primarily **[[because]{.underline}]{.mark}** **["for man [the other
has a captivating value]{.mark},]{.underline}** **[on account of the
anticipation that is represented by the unitary image]{.underline}**
**[as perceived]{.underline}** either in the mirror or in the entire
reality of the fellow being" (I: 125). **[This captation by the
other]{.underline}** **[is also the process of the birth of the bodily
ego,]{.underline}** which is always mediated by desire. **[The
subject]{.underline}** **[originally]{.underline}** **[locates and
recognises]{.underline}** **[desire through the intermediary not only of
his own image, but of the body of his fellow being. [It's]{.mark}
exactly [at that moment]{.mark} [that]{.mark} the human being's
[consciousness]{.mark}]{.underline}**, in the form of consciousness of
self, **[[distinguishes itself]{.mark}.]{.underline}** It is in so far
**[[as he recognises]{.mark} [his desire]{.mark} [in the body of the
other]{.mark} [that the exchange takes place]{.mark}]{.underline}**. It
is in so far as **[his desire has gone over to the other side that he
assimilates himself to the body of the other and recognises himself as
body]{.underline}**. (I: 147) **[Identification,]{.underline}** then**[,
is always mediated by desire,]{.underline}**13 **[and clearly, one's own
desire emerges in the Other]{.underline}**. One consequence of this
formulation is that the body is "factitious" (I: 147), insofar as human
consciousness, which is bound to it, can nevertheless conceive of itself
as distinct. Also, this ability of man to conceive of himself "as other
than he is...entirely structures his fantasy life" (I: 79). The body is
constituted in a see-saw movement of desire. In fact, **[there can be no
imaginary relation or ego function without desire]{.underline}**, and
**[[when]{.mark} we invoke the term "[desire,"]{.mark} [we are
always]{.mark} [in the realm of]{.mark} language and the
[symbolic]{.mark}]{.underline}**. **[The symbolic, then, is pivotal for
the very existence of the mirror stage.]{.underline}** The optical
experiment that Lacan invokes to clarify the mirror stage concept
emphasizes the importance of perspective. It is possible that the image
may not be successfully produced if the mirror were to be inclined in
one way or another. For Lacan, this is an indication of "the uneasy
accommodation of the imaginary in man" (I: 140). As he says, "everything
depends on the position of the subject. And **[the position of the
subject]{.underline}**...**[is]{.underline}** essentially
**[characterised by its place in the symbolic world,]{.underline}** in
other words in **[the world of speech"]{.underline}** (I: 80). This
schema then suggests that **[[neither the imaginary nor the mirror stage
can]{.mark} legitimately [claim]{.mark} an [anteriority]{.mark} to the
symbolic]{.underline}**. Even though we tend to schematize "psychical
development" chronologically, probing the structural relation of the
three levels of **[the imaginary, the symbolic and the
Real]{.underline}** reveals that they **[are imbricated in one
another]{.underline}** in an inextricable fashion. In other words, it's
the symbolic relation which defines the position of the subject as
seeing. **[It is speech, [the symbolic relation]{.mark}, which
[determines the]{.mark} greater or lesser [degree of]{.mark} perfection,
of [completeness,]{.mark} of approximation, [of the
imaginary]{.mark}.]{.underline}** **[This representation allows us to
draw the distinction between the]{.underline}** Idealich and the
Ichideal, between the **[ideal ego and the egoideal.]{.underline}**
**[The ego-ideal governs the interplay of relations on which all
relations with others depend]{.underline}**. (I: 141, emphasis added)
**[The interplay of relations]{.underline}** with others **[is governed
by desire]{.underline}**---**[a desire that is always of the
Other]{.underline}**. **[[Desire must be understood]{.mark} in its
[mediating]{.mark} role bet[ween the image of the other and the
consciousness of one's own body]{.mark} which the image
engenders]{.underline}**. That is, **[[the subject cannot
recognize]{.mark} his/her [desire except as manifested by the
other]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[as his/her own "ideal
form"]{.underline}** (I: 176). **[Thus it is always alienated from the
subject.]{.underline}** In the human subject, desire is realised in the
other, by the other.... That is the second moment, the specular moment,
the moment when **[the subject]{.underline}** has integrated the form of
the ego. But he **[is only capable of integrating]{.underline}** it
**[after a first swing of the see-saw when he had precisely exchanged
his ego for this other desire which he sees in the other]{.underline}**.
From then on, **[the desire of the other]{.underline}**, **[which is
man's desire enters into the mediation of language]{.underline}**. It is
in the other, by \[par\] the other, that desire is named**[. It enters
into the symbolic relation of I and you]{.underline}**, **[in relation
of mutual recognition]{.underline}** and transcendence, into the order
of a law which is already quite ready to encompass the history of each
individual. (I: 177) Insofar as **[the subject as ego cannot come into
existence except in a symbolic relation]{.underline}** (**[which always
implies desire]{.underline}**), **[it is logically impossible to isolate
the imaginary or ego function from the realm of the
signifies]{.underline}** **[Thus to speak of
identification]{.underline}**---sexual, **[racial]{.underline}**,
etc.---in **[terms of a mirror relation]{.underline}**, **[as pure
image, is to produce an untenable opposition between the image and the
signifier]{.underline}**, or a specious sense of "development" from the
imaginary to the symbolic. Consequently, the body cannot be pure image,
an ego identification produced in the mirror of error; it is, as Bruce
Fink has insisted, "written with signifiers and is thus foreign, Other"
(1995:12). **[[The signifier that constitutes the]{.mark} subject's
[body emerges from the Other]{.mark} as the expression of recognition or
ratification of the mirror image, thereby enabling the image to be
introjected]{.underline}**. The imaginary body has a symbolic status.
**[[As a creature of desire]{.mark}, [the subject invests in the
signifier]{.mark} which marks it, and locates it within a matrix where
"the body" is engendered in submission to the logic of the
signifier.]{.underline}** **[[Thus to believe that we "have" particular
bodies]{.mark} on the basis of a simple visibility [is to ignore
the]{.mark} function of the [signifier]{.mark} in the production of the
seeing subject]{.underline}**. Thus **[the subject's corporeality is
itself constituted as]{.underline}** a **[coherent]{.underline}** image
**[through the intervention of the signifier]{.underline}**. As Lacan
recommends, "One should always work at the level of the alphabet" (I:
35). In other words, **[we must hunt down the signifier behind the image
if we wish to discern the subject of race,]{.underline}** or to put it
even more precisely, we must grasp the contours of the lack that the
signifier stands for, which in turn supports the body image.

#### Racism is not a totalizing dialectic, but operates through regimes of facialization. White supremacy silences race's threatening viscosity by confining it to static definitions of oppression. The aff flows like water. 

Arun **Saldanha 6** \[Professor of Social Theory and the University of
Minnesota, "Reontologising race: the machinic geography of phenotype",
Environment and Planning D: Society and Space 2006, volume 24, pages
9-24\]//Lex RA

Spatiality, viscosity, machinism There is an explicit or implicit notion
of space imbricated in any ontology. Thus, Doreen Massey has
consistently attacked the static, nonrelational conception of space of
the modern masculine tradition (Massey, 2005). But neither is the
spatiality Massey defends one of pure flux, in which there are no
\`points\' (compare Doel, 1998). **[What is needed is a concept of space
in which fixity can emerge from flux under certain
conditions]{.underline}**. This is **[different from]{.underline}**
saying that there is **[perpetual vacillation]{.underline}** at infinite
speed **[between two poles,]{.underline}** **[fixity and
flow]{.underline}**, like in some postmodern dialectic or like Derridean
undecidability, even if the poles are effects of the vacillation. Real
process is not vacillary; it is irreversible and messy. **[To evoke the
continuous but constrained dynamism of space, I want to propose the
figure of viscosity. [Neither perfectly fluid nor solid, the viscous
invokes surface tension]{.mark} and resistance to perturbation and
mixing. Viscosity means]{.underline}** that the **[physical
characteristics of a substance explain its unique
movements.]{.underline}** **[[There are local]{.underline}]{.mark}** and
**[[temporary thickenings of interacting bodies, which then collectively
become sticky]{.mark}, capable of c[apturing more bodies like
them:]{.mark}]{.underline}** an emergent slime mold. **[[Under]{.mark}
certain [circumstances, the collectivity dissolves]{.mark}, the
constituent bodies flowing freely again. [The world is an immense mass
of viscosities]{.mark}]{.underline}**, becoming thicker here, and
thinner there. In Genesis, Michel Serres tries to understand emergence,
how unity can emerge from the background noise of multiplicity. At one
point he writes of the \`\`chain of contingency\'\': \`\`No, the
contingent chain does not break, its links slide over one another, as
though viscous. They touch because they are adjacent, they touch like
sailors\' hitches or the loops of motorway cloverleaves are stacked upon
one another. It is not a linkage, but a local pull, by way of little
frictions. The local pull induces global movement very seldom, although
it can happen. This is not a solid chain, it is simply a liquid
movement, a viscosity, a propagation that wagers its age in each
locality\'\' (\[1982\] 1995, page 71, his italics). [**[Race must
similarly be conceived as a chain of contingency,]{.underline}** **[in
which]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[the [connections between]{.mark} its
[constituent components]{.mark} [are not given,
but]{.mark}]{.underline}** are made **[[viscous through local
attractions.]{.mark} [Whiteness]{.mark},]{.underline}** for example,
**[[is about the sticky connections between property, privilege,
and]{.mark} a paler [skin]{.mark}. [There is no essence of whiteness,
but]{.mark}]{.underline}** there is **[[a relative fixity]{.mark} that
inheres in all the \`local pulls\' of its many elements in
flux.]{.underline}** **[Emergence and viscosity are
complementary]{.underline}** concepts, **[the first pertaining to the
genesis of distinctions, the second to the modality]{.underline}** of
that genesis. **[Race\'s spatiality is emphatically not about discrete
separations between \`races\'. [Nobody \`has\' a race, but bodies are
racialised]{.mark}.]{.underline}** Gilroy asks: **[\`\`if \`race\' is a
useful way]{.underline}** 18 A Saldanha **[of classifying people, then
[how many \`races\' are there]{.mark}?]{.underline}**\'\' (2000, page
37). **[This question [betrays a logic of solids and
grids]{.mark}.]{.underline}** The concept of race is not for taxonomic
ordering, but for studying the movements between human bodies, things,
and their changing environment. The concept of race is like the concept
of subculture, or diseasenobody wants to know how many subcultures or
diseases there are, but how they come to be. **[What [are the
constituent components of race? Potentially
everything]{.mark},]{.underline}** but certainly strands of **[[DNA,
phenotyp]{.mark}ical variation, [discursive practice]{.mark}s (law,
media, science), [artefacts]{.mark}]{.underline}** such as clothes and
food, **[and]{.underline}** the distribution of
**[[wealth]{.underline}]{.mark}**. How these are connected is entirely
immanent to the way certain humans behave in certain circumstances.
Sarah **[Whatmore]{.underline}** (2002) **[might call
race]{.underline}** intrinsically **[more-than-human, [irreducible to
either biology or culture]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Deleuze and
Guattari\'s A Thousand Plateaus can be understood as a conceptualisation
of irreducible and immanent heterogeneities like race (though they do
not explicitly confirm that race is such a heterogeneity). **[They call
these heterogeneities [machinic assemblages]{.mark}.]{.underline}**
**[\`\`Taking the feudal assemblage as an example, we would have to
consider the interminglings of bodies defining feudalism: the body of
the earth]{.underline}** and **[the social body]{.underline}**; the body
of the **[overlord,]{.underline}** the **[vassal,]{.underline}** the
**[serf]{.underline}**; the body of **[the knight]{.underline}** and
**[the horse]{.underline}** and their new relation to the stirrup;
**[the weapons]{.underline}** and tools **[assuring a symbiosis
of]{.underline}** bodiesa whole **[machinic assemblage]{.underline}**.
We would also have to consider statements, expressions, the juridical
regime of heraldry, all of the incorporeal transformations, in
particular, oaths and their variables (the oath of obedience, but also
the oath of love, etc.): the collective assemblage of enunciation. On
the other axis, we would have to consider the feudal territorialities
and reterritorializations, and at the same time the line of
deterritorialization that carries away both the knight and his mount,
statements and acts. We would have to consider how all this combines in
the Crusades\'\' (Deleuze and Guattari, \[1980\] 1987, page 89). **[A
machinic geography of bodies asks what immanent connections they forge
with things and places,]{.underline}** how they work, travel, fight,
write, lovehow **[these [bodies become viscous, slow down]{.mark}, get
[into certain habits]{.mark}, into certain
[collectivities]{.mark}]{.underline}**, like city, social stratum, or
**[[racial formation]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Machinism is wary of
mediation: it prefers connections and viscosities. **[Machinism asks how
incredibly diverse processes]{.underline}** (such as agriculture and
sexuality, religion and property law) **[interlock, [like cogs and
wheels instead of signifiers and signifieds]{.mark}.]{.underline}** But
machinism is not physicalism. It understands entities not as perfectly
knowable cause \^ effect sequences, but as bundles of virtual
capacities. Approaching phenotype machinically means being prepared for
the unpreparable: **[phenotype connects in infinite ways.]{.underline}**
Living, social machines are not machines in the narrow sense, because
they lack a preconceived \`function\' and are constantly evolving. **[A
quick return to Fanon to elucidate the machinic assemblage of
race.]{.underline}** Another well-known quote: \`\`The native town is a
hungry town, starved of bread, of meat, of shoes, of coal, of light. The
native town is a crouching village, a town on its knees, a town
wallowing in the mire. It is a town of niggers and dirty arabs. The look
that the native turns on the settler\'s town is a look of lust, a look
of envy; it expresses his dreams of possessionall manner of possession:
to sit at the settler\'s table, to sleep in the settler\'s bed, with his
wife if possible. The colonized man is an envious man. And this the
settler knows very well; when their glances meet he ascertains bitterly,
always on the defensive \`They want to take our place\'. It is true, for
there is no native who does not dream at least once a day of setting
himself up in the settler\'s place\'\' (\[1961\] 1967, page 30).
Reontologising race 19 Numerous **[authors have theorised about the
intersection of possession, sexuality, urbanism and race]{.underline}**
(for example Low, 1996). **[[The machinic geography]{.mark} of
phenotype, however, [takes issue with the Hegelian self/other
scheme]{.mark} [that supports much of \[Fanon's\] work, and studies
instead how certain bodies stick to certain
spaces]{.mark},]{.underline}** how they are **[chained by hunger,
cold,]{.underline}** darkness, mud, poverty, crime, glances **[full of
envy and anxiety]{.underline}**. **[[The segregation between colonists
and colonised is the]{.underline}]{.mark}** apparently **[[binary
result]{.mark} of many]{.underline}** nitty-gritty **[material
processes]{.underline}** **[which, [when analysed, render it a lot less
binary]{.mark}]{.underline}**. This also means **[race is devious in
inventing new ways of chaining bodies. Race is creative, constantly
morphing,]{.underline}** now disguised as sexual desire, now as la
mission civilatrice, all the while weaving new elements in its wake.
Deleuze and Guattari might say that **[[what defines race is not
rigidity]{.underline}]{.mark}** or inevitability, **[[but its \`\`lines
of flight\'\']{.mark}.]{.underline}** Race can be as stark as apartheid,
but mostly it is fuzzy and operates through something else. The social
sciences literature on race (urban geography, postcolonial theory, film
studies) remains relevant from the machinic perspective. **[[Race is
shown to exist through ghettoes, travel writing, and Hollywood
cinema]{.mark}. What this literature shows is precisely race\'s plastic,
emergent, and more-than-human spatiality]{.underline}** (for example,
Anderson, 1991; hooks, 1992; Jackson and Penrose, 1993; Jacobs, 1996;
Robinson, 1996). Still, more geographical and anthropological work needs
to be done to theorise the biocultural imbrications of race. For
example, in the introduction to the collection Race, Nature and the
Politics of Difference it is argued that \`\`both
**[[race]{.mark}]{.underline}** and nature
**[[are]{.mark}]{.underline}** historical artefacts: **[[assemblages of
material, discourse, and practice irreducible to a single timeless
essence]{.mark}.]{.underline}** By charting the ways in which race and
nature work together, and by tracing key disruptions in their busy
traffic, we emphasize the cultural labors required to maintain them as
they are. \... **[[We write against racisms -- not against
\`race\']{.mark} [but against the exclusionary effects produced through
its invocation]{.mark}, deployment, and reproduction\'\']{.underline}**
(Moore et al, 2003, page 42). But the full implications of the term
assemblagethat it includes biological and other nonhuman forcesstill
need to be explored. There is some work being done that quietly
disrespects the disciplinary boundaries of modern epistemology.
Anthropologically inclined medical research has the potential to offer a
critical approach to the biocultural aspects of racial division (Wade,
2002, pages 117 \^ 122). Luca Cavalli-Sforza maps human migration using
genetics and physical anthropology as well as archaeology, linguistics,
and history (Cavalli-Sforza et al, 1994). This research deserves
theoretical attention, so that more rigorous studies of the discursive,
technological, and economic embeddedness of phenotype can be imagined.
Zack (2002) has recently argued that physical anthropology can only
account for variation through heredity, not the folk (taxonomic)
conception of race as such. She therefore continues to define \`race\'
as an essentialist social construction which has no basis in the science
of phenotype. **[[What is needed]{.underline}]{.mark}**, however, **[[is
to highjack the]{.mark} folk c[onception]{.mark} and rethink race as
culturally embedded phenotype.]{.underline}** **[Saying that race has no
basis in biology is different]{.underline}** from saying that phenotype
plays some role in racial differentiation. **[[Phenotype is a crucial
element]{.mark} in the assemblage called race,]{.underline}** **[and,
[because phenotype is already]{.mark} nondiscrete and [shaped by
culture, race cannot be an essentialist concept]{.mark}.]{.underline}**
**[Now, [what does this nonessentialism mean to antiracist
politics?]{.mark}]{.underline}** A thousand tiny races **[[Every time
phenotype makes another machinic connection, there is a stutter. Every
time bodies are further entrenched in segregation]{.mark}, however
brutal, [there needs to be an affective investment of some sort. This is
the ruptural moment in which to intervene]{.mark}.]{.underline}** 20 A
Saldanha **[[Race should not be eliminated, but proliferated,]{.mark}
[its]{.mark} many [energies directed at multiplying racial
differences]{.mark} so as [to render them joyfully
cacophonic]{.mark}.]{.underline}** Many in American critical race theory
also argue against a utopian transcendence of race, taking from W E B Du
Bois and pragmatism a reflexive, sometimes strategically nationalist
attitude towards racial embodiment (compare Outlaw, 1996; Shuford, 2001;
Winant, 2004). **[[What is needed is an affirmation
of]{.mark}]{.underline}** race\'s creativity and virtuality: **[[what

far-from-equilibrium, [liberatory.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[[It is not
that everyone becomes]{.mark}]{.underline}** completely Brownian (or
[**[brown]{.underline}**!]{.mark}), completely **[[similar,
or]{.underline}]{.mark}** completely **[[unique. It
